While V1 completed configuration initialization within the main function, V2 follows a completely different code flow. Unlike V1, variable names in V2 are not obfuscated, and the configuration data is stored as plain strings in the Settings class rather than being AES-encrypted. This unencrypted approach is consistent across both trial and production builds.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-07/image15.png.webp?itok=VaiZaoxA 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-07/image15.png.webp?itok=CpxRW9_b 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":912,"height":581},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-07/image15.png.webp?itok=KHbQXFjo 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-07/image15.png.webp?itok=645JPWxR 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":890,"height":567},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/image15.png.webp?itok=3K1hliHX 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-07/image15.png.webp?itok=1HeuOwHv 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":690,"height":440}],"caption":"Configuration values of Neptune RAT V2 Sample","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/image15.png.webp?itok=3K1hliHX","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConclusion\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eWe identified Neptune RAT configurations within our historical XWORM tracking data, which prompted the hypothesis that these two malware strains might be linked. OSINT investigations uncovered some tentative connections, including forks of the \u003cem\u003eMasonGroup/NeptuneRatV1\u003c/em\u003e repository. Although the original repository is no longer accessible, these forks contained code heavily overlapping with XWORM. Notably, the current Neptune RAT builder references the Freemasonry group, aligning with the Discord server identified in our research.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eReverse engineering Neptune RAT V1 revealed further compelling similarities. While the shared resource structure might be dismissed as a coincidence, given that many .NET RATs use similar frameworks, the resource encryption scheme is essentially identical in both: AES encryption in insecure ECB mode combined with the same key-derivation method using a hard-coded mutex. Their persistence mechanisms are also functionally and structurally alike. Additionally, the clipper functionality employs the same regular expressions and targets the same cryptocurrencies. Although these expressions focus on legacy Bitcoin addresses rather than newer standards like Bech32, this link is somewhat weak given the widespread use of these expressions in malware.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe strong similarities between XWORM and Neptune RAT V1, contrasted with their clear divergence from other open-source RATs, strongly suggest that Neptune RAT V1 is related to XWORM. However, these parallels do not extend to Neptune RAT V2, whose trial version, while functionally similar to V1, appears to be a complete code rewrite.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eList of lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLarge language models (LLMs) are everywhere. Since OpenAI’s ChatGPT burst onto the scene a few years ago, these tools have completely reshaped how we search, ask questions and navigate the web. They have quickly woven themselves into our daily routines, helping us build grocery lists, find restaurant recommendations and even plan entire vacations.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd all of this? It seems to come at no cost. Just create an account and start using platforms like ChatGPT (OpenAI), Gemini (Google) or DeepSeek. But that leads us to an important question: are these tools truly free? And if not, what is the real price we are paying?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eTo find out, we dug into the privacy policies of the top AI platforms, so you do not have to. Here is what we uncovered about the actual cost of using today’s most popular LLMs.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003ePrivacy by design, and other nice stories\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLet’s walk through a familiar scenario: planning your next vacation. Like many of us, you want the perfect destination, at the perfect time, all within a specific budget. That means searching for cheap flights, affordable hotels and ideally, something close to the city center that still feels like a getaway.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eIn the past, you might have spent hours, or days, browsing search engines, comparing options, reading reviews and hunting for deals. It is time-consuming and, frankly, a bit overwhelming.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBut now? Who has time for all that? More importantly – why bother when your friendly AI assistant can handle it for you? All you need to do is head to your favorite large language model and type in a well-crafted prompt:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e“Hello, I want to go for vacation on august somewhere sunny. I would like a beautiful island somewhere in the Mediterranean Sea with plenty of activities and tasty food. I do not want it to be too crowded and not extremely expensive. Find me also flights from Oslo to this destination that are cheap or tell me which dates I should look for. Finally, find me a nice hotel 3 stars and above with a room for 2 adults and 2 kids. Find something that is bellow 150 euros per day with availability for 6 days, please.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWith a prompt like that, the model gets to work. Within seconds, it returns tailored recommendations that check all your boxes. Even better? It might offer to book the flights and hotel for you – asking for your credit card and passport details so you don’t have to lift a finger. Just sit back, relax and dream of sun-drenched beaches.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eYou may even notice a few quick notifications as the system says things like “memory updated”, but nothing else seems to change, so no need to worry… right?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLet’s take a moment to unpack what happened behind the scenes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhen we typed our prompt, the model did not just give us a list of results – it broke down our request into smaller, more specific queries. It recognized that we are planning a vacation, live in Oslo, have a partner and two kids, prefer nice hotels, and are working with a budget of €150 per night. Piece by piece, it started building a profile of who we are and what we like.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThat profile does not just disappear. It is often stored, somewhere.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd if we take a closer look at the privacy policies of major AI providers, we start to see how that information can be used. So, let’s break it down: what exactly is being collected, and how is it being used?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eChatGPT by OpenAI\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLet’s start with the elephant in the room: OpenAI, the most popular and widely used large language model provider.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAccording to its privacy policy, OpenAI collects a wide range of personal data. This includes:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAccount information\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUser content (everything you type)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eCommunication details\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eOther information you voluntarily provide\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLog data and usage patterns\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eDevice and location data\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eCookies and similar tracking technologies\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eInformation received from third-party sources\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eIn short: it is not just your prompts that are being collected. It is your device, your location, how you interact with the model and even data that can be bought or received from other sources.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBut what is even more important than what is collected is how it is used. OpenAI states that this data helps them “provide, analyze and maintain” their services, “improve and develop” new features, “communicate with users” and “prevent misuse or fraud.” All of which sound standard, until you read the fine print about data sharing.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAccording to the policy, OpenAI may share your information, with broad discretion, for purposes such as:\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cul\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eStrategic transactions (e.g., mergers or acquisitions)\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBusiness operations\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eLegal compliance\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003cli\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd more vaguely, with “affiliates”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\u003c/ul\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eSo, what does this mean in practice?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eOnce you provide your data, it is no longer truly yours. It can be used to train future models, shared with business partners or even leveraged in a corporate acquisition. And the kicker? This applies whether you are a free user or a paying subscriber.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eYour data powers the system, but it does not belong to you anymore.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eGemini by Google\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eNext, let’s look at how the most popular search engine’s AI model, Gemini by Google, handles user privacy. Unsurprisingly, the situation is very similar to what we saw with OpenAI.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eGemini collects not just your chats and interactions, but also any files, images or browser page content you share. It logs your location, IP address, submitted feedback and even physical addresses, like home or work, if they are part of your input.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhen it comes to how this data is used, the language is broad and vague. Google states that your data helps them “provide, improve, develop and personalize” their services and machine learning technologies. But if you dig deeper into their multi-layered privacy policy, a clearer picture emerges: your personal information can also be used for targeted advertising and to make recommendations for other Google products.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd it gets more concerning.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eGoogle explicitly warns users not to share personal or confidential data in Gemini chats. Why? Because human reviewers, known as annotators, may read your conversations, access your files and use that data to improve future model performance. So, not only does your data no longer belong to you; it may also be read and evaluated by actual people.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eOnce again, it does not matter whether you are a free user or a paid subscriber. The privacy terms apply across the board. From the moment you interact with Gemini, your data becomes part of its ecosystem.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eDeepSeek: The new kid on the block\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eFinally, we reviewed the privacy policy of DeepSeek, a platform that gained major traction last year after claiming OpenAI-level performance with just a fraction of the resources. It quickly rose in popularity, eventually topping Apple’s App Store charts.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eDeepSeek’s privacy policy appears standard. They collect personal data associated with your account, your input and any details you provide when contacting them. But as with the other major players, a deeper dive reveals a long list of data points they track device model, IP address, identifiers, location, cookies, payment details, text inputs, uploaded files, chat history, user age, contact information and more.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhere things get even more concerning is where your data goes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUnlike OpenAI or Google, DeepSeek transfers user data from Europe and the U.S. to servers in China, placing it under Chinese authority. This means your information is no longer protected by EU or U.S. privacy laws. According to Chinese regulations, the government may access or request user data at any time, for any reason – with little transparency or recourse.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAs for how the data is used, the story remains familiar. DeepSeek claims your information helps them improve, develop and train their models, whether by feeding prompts directly into training datasets or by analyzing usage patterns. They also cite compliance with legal obligations and protecting the “interests of users and others”, though who those “others” are is never clearly defined.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAnd, of course, they reserve the right to share your personal data with third-party partners who help operate or develop their services.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe bottom line? No matter which platform you choose, your data stops being just yours the moment you use the service.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eHow privacy has changed\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eSome might argue that privacy concerns are not new, we have been sharing data online long before large language models (LLMs) took over. After all, even planning a vacation used to involve multiple search queries typed into our favorite search engine. So, what is the difference?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThat argument seems fair. But there is a key distinction.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBefore LLMs, we were in control. Searching for cheap flights or hotel deals meant navigating between websites of our choosing. We could decide which platforms to interact with, and, at least to some extent, who saw our data. Tools like \u003ca href=\"https://us.norton.com/products/norton-antitrack\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"\u003eNorton AntiTrack\u003c/a\u003e made it possible to keep trackers at bay, allowing us to interact directly with service providers while limiting what the search engine itself could collect.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eNow, that dynamic has shifted.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWhen we use LLMs, the model is not just answering a question, it is becoming the gateway to everything. It decides which services to show, which sources to pull from and how to summarize or filter the information. You no longer browse freely; you are handed a curated response. And in the process, the LLM provider sees everything, from your preferences and prompts to your files, location and interactions.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eEven more troubling? You cannot always tell which sources were used or whether the results are truly in your best interest.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eSo, does this mean privacy is a lost game in the age of LLMs?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe answer is not simple.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eCountermeasures\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUnfortunately, there is no silver bullet when it comes to protecting your privacy while using large language models. But that does not mean you are completely powerless; there are a few settings and habits that can help minimize your data exposure. Let’s take a closer look at what is possible, starting with the most popular platform:\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eChatGPT by OpenAI\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eIn OpenAI’s ChatGPT, privacy-conscious users can adjust a few settings to gain more control over how their data is used. To access them, click on your avatar in the top right corner and open Settings. From there, navigate to the Data Controls tab, where you can toggle off “Improve the model for everyone.” This setting tells OpenAI not to use your chats to train future models.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eYou can also visit the Personalization tab and disable memory features to stop the model from saving and recalling information about you. If you are curious about what the model has already learned, click “Manage Memories” to review and delete anything it has inferred.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eKeep in mind, disabling memory means a less personalized experience, so you may receive less accurate or relevant responses to your prompts. And unfortunately, this does not mean you are fully in the clear: even with all settings turned off, the personal and sensitive information you share may still be collected and stored by OpenAI.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eBottom line: these settings offer a degree of control, but not full privacy.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eGemini by Google\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eWith Gemini, your options to control privacy are a bit more limited. The main setting available is found by clicking on Activity in the bottom left panel, where you can disable it. However, this does not mean your data will not be stored or processed, it only means that human reviewers will not inspect the information you share.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eGemini’s privacy policy does not explicitly say whether your data is still used to train models without human review, so it remains unclear if your chats contribute to future model training behind the scenes.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUnlike ChatGPT, Gemini does not automatically infer information about you. Instead, the model only learns personal details if you explicitly provide them. If you want to delete stored information, you can either switch off Activity altogether or delete entries individually within the same tab.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eStill, there is no true way to avoid sharing data with Google, and once you share your information, it no longer belongs to you.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eDeepSeek\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe situation with DeepSeek is a bit grimmer. If you click the bottom left corner and then go to Settings, you will find the Profile tab. Here, the only privacy control available is the option to switch off “Improve model for everyone.”\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eDeepSeek claims that they do not use your content to train their models if you disable this setting, but beyond that, their policy is vague and unclear. What is clear, however, is that once you share your data, it no longer truly belongs to you.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eWhat does the future bring?\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eAI-powered services like these are here to stay, and they simply cannot operate without the vast amounts of data generated by their users. For now, there are few regulations or safeguards to limit how this data is collected, stored or used, which means privacy is taking a backseat.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eUntil stronger protections are put in place, the responsibility falls on users to safeguard their own sensitive information. That means sharing as little personal data as possible, especially when planning something like a vacation. Otherwise, you might just find your itinerary showing up in someone else’s AI-generated recommendations in the next version of the model you use.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp lang=\"EN-US\"\u003eThe future of privacy in AI is not set in stone yet. But one thing is certain: staying informed and cautious is your best defence.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"}],"itemState":"published","timeToRead":"13","translations":[],"originalLanguagePost":{},"mediaType":{"video":false,"audio":false},"socialMediaMetaImage":"https://www.gendigital.com/sites/default/files/styles/social_media_meta/public/2025-07/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_347002.jpg?h=9da44f55\u0026itok=1IpsYFX7","language":"en","heroImage":{"default":{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_hero_extra_wide/public/2025-07/Stocksy_txpe08a537dYqu300_OriginalDelivery_347002.jpg.webp?itok=wyIHNswN isn't what it used to be, it’s gotten a corporate makeover. What began as a work of lone hackers, has evolved into a sophisticated business model known as Ransomware-as-a-Service (RaaS). In this setup, ransomware developers and a network of affiliates collaborate like any legitimate enterprise (minus the part where they extort people).\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eUnlike traditional companies, ransomware groups don't publish earning reports or financial disclosures. Their operations are covered in shadows - until something goes wrong. Occasionally, these groups become targets themselves, and when they do, we get a rare glimpse behind the curtain.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThis article explores one such moment. On May 7th 2025, arguably the most notorious ransomware group, LockBit, had its panel defaced, exposing a link to a dump of its internal database. The data revealed that the panel had been in operation since at least December 18th, 2024, based on early test entries in the database. While the leak has drawn attention for its technical and operational revelations, this analysis takes a different approach: it focuses on the economic structure behind LockBit's operations. How LockBit recruits affiliates, manages operations, and generates profit. In doing so, we aim to shed light on the inner workings of the underground economy of cybercrime.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch2\u003eInside the LockBit Panel\u0026nbsp;\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe leaked database revealed a sophisticated backend system - basically, a control panel for managing a global extortion operation. It included everything from user accounts and victim profiles to ransomware build information and chat messages exchanged between affiliates and victims.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThrough this panel, affiliates had access to generate customized ransomware payloads for different environments such as Windows, Linux, and ESXi. The system also stored detailed information about LockBit's victims (or \"clients\" as they are called internally). Each victim had an assigned public key, responsible affiliate, a payment status, and a complete chat history with the affiliate including features like message read receipts.\u0026nbsp;\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eOne particularly eyebrow-rasing detail: affiliate passwords were stored in plaintext. A surprising move for a group that claims to be all about operational control. But hey, even cybercrime syndicates cut corners on cybersecurity.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eEssentially, we got a preview of a modern CMR (Customer Relationship Management) system - this time not for selling products or services, but rather for extortion.\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=XsZa5cf9 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=Vm-OtMgy 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":1170,"height":685},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=zWoqbMKA 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=xRTe5hZ4 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":890,"height":521},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=noGYbYcb 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=Roix18ht 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":690,"height":404}],"caption":"Schema of the database and the most interesting attributes","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/lockbit-dump-erd.png.webp?itok=noGYbYcb","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003ch2\u003eA Day in the Life of a LockBit Affiliate\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eAffiliates are individuals enrolled in the LockBit's affiliate program, think of them as franchises in the ransomware world. After gaining access to the panel, they are granted permissions to build specific versions of the LockBit ransomware. These builds are then deployed to victim systems, either through self-obtained access or via third-party brokers.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhen a victim is compromised, they are directed to LockBit's infrastructure, where they land in a private chatroom assigned to the affiliate who infected them. This is where real business begins: negotiation, manipulation and psychological warfare.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eWhile affiliates often present themselves as cold and transactional, the chat logs reveal a more manipulative side. Affiliates frequently use everything, from manufactured urgency to subtle threats, designed to wear victims down and push them towards payment.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-07/LocBit%20Ransomware%20Negotiation%20Transcript%20%281%29.png.webp?itok=BdaY4F8h a ransom is agreed upon, the affiliate generates a new Bitcoin wallet (for our case, we will call it a \u003cem\u003eransom wallet\u003c/em\u003e) to receive the payment. Those wallets are created and controlled by the affiliate, and thus not directly stored in the LockBit panel database. They can be found in the negotiation chats.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eAfter receiving the ransom, the affiliate is expected to send 20% of the ransom to a \u003cem\u003ecommission wallet\u003c/em\u003e (a wallet address that is pre-generated and stored in the panel for each affiliate-victim pair). These commission wallets are under LockBit's control and serve as the group's cut of the ransom payments.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe affiliates' share (80%) is usually moved quickly through additional wallets or cryptomixers (services designed to obscure origin of funds), the commission wallets remain largely untouched. The fee for aspiring affiliates ranges from around $700 to $810. After transferring this fee, LockBit creates a new user in the database and sets them the role of newbie. Then, the affiliate officially gets access to the panel and is able to deploy LockBit ransomware and use their infrastructure for ransom negotiations. Startup costs, criminal edition.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe dump contained more than 3,600 invitations, with wallets designed to pay the fee either through Bitcoin or Monero. Because of the difficult traceability of the Monero wallets, we cannot say for sure the total number of panel fee payments. But among the invitations that used Bitcoin, only 12 users actually paid the invitation fee during the operation of the panel.\u003c/p\u003e\u003ch3\u003eThe Revenue Picture\u003c/h3\u003e\u003cp\u003eEvery LockBit client has an entry in the panel's table. The group collects data about the victim's website, their expected revenue, whether they are an important client or no or their assigned master public key (the tables in the database also show column for private key, however, it is empty for every victim). Since December 2024, the database contained in total of 246 victims with 208 that actually connected to the dedicated chat and started with the negotations. However, from examining the commission and ransom wallets, only 19 paid the ransom payment. The total amount of ransom extorted was 24.8 BTC ($2,660,484 by June 26th, 2025), from which 20% went to the LockBit operators and 80% to the affiliates.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe following figures describe the timeline with the amount of LockBit's commissions (20% commission part that went to LockBit) and number of successfully attacked victims with ransom being paid.\u003c/p\u003e"},{"bundle":"image","images":[{"sources":[{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large/public/2025-07/lockbit_earnings.png.webp?itok=mVxwOhVg 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_large_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit_earnings.png.webp?itok=isUhnufC 2x","media":"(min-width: 1020px)","type":"image/webp","width":1170,"height":776},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium/public/2025-07/lockbit_earnings.png.webp?itok=Bmk78UZz 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_medium_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit_earnings.png.webp?itok=jMOPBzd2 2x","media":"(min-width: 720px)","type":"image/webp","width":890,"height":590},{"srcset":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/lockbit_earnings.png.webp?itok=BWdpmcgB 1x, https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small_2x/public/2025-07/lockbit_earnings.png.webp?itok=NeZkJKs9 2x","media":"(min-width: 420px)","type":"image/webp","width":690,"height":458}],"caption":"LockBit earnings and timeline of victims","url":"https://www.gendigital.com/blog/sites/default/files/styles/blogs_paragraph_image_small/public/2025-07/lockbit_earnings.png.webp?itok=BWdpmcgB","link":""}]},{"bundle":"rich_text","content":"\u003cp\u003eIn total, there were 40 active affiliates registered in the panel. Thoughts: Crime as Business\u003c/h2\u003e\u003cp\u003eLockBit’s panel leak is one of the clearest examples we’ve seen on Ransomware-as-a-Service functioning like a tech startup, except its “value proposition” is fear, pressure and extortion.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eThe business was powered by a surprisingly systematic affiliate model, built-in infrastructure, and financial incentives. Despite the flood of invitations and flashy promises, the majority of affiliates never got paid. Of the 246 victims listed, just 19 were paid, suggesting most people either lacked the funds or simply refused to play along. If this all sounds like a parody of a VC-backed startup pitch, it kind of is. But the impact is real, and the profits (while smaller than the headlines suggest) are still enough to fund the next wave of extortion innovation.\u003c/p\u003e\u003cp\u003eFor readers interested in a
